يصح
His statement: (Whoever stops at Arafah for a moment from zawal to fajr of the day of nahr, his Hajj is complete, even if ignorant, asleep, or unconscious) because the Prophet (peace be upon him) stopped after zawal and said, 'Whoever catches Arafah by night has caught the Hajj.' So his action was a clarification of its beginning time, and his statement a clarification of its end. And by 'moment' is meant the customary moment/span, which is a small amount of time, and it is the interpretation when used by jurists, not the hour of astronomers as we explained in Hayd. And by 'completion of Hajj by wuquf' in the hadith and their expressions is meant safety from invalidity, not its reality, since the second pillar remains, which is tawaf. And it indicates that intention is not a condition for the validity of wuquf; it is restricted because tawaf requires intention, so that even if one tawafs fleeing from something... And the difference between them is that tawaf is an intended act of worship, therefore one may perform it as a supererogatory act, so it is necessary to stipulate intention for its validity. And my saying 'night of the moon' I followed his statement in al-Durr 'night of the full moon', and similar is what passed in al-Arjuzah, and apparently what is meant is the night of the 13th because the pilgrim does not stay in Mina after it. Contemplate. ... [section on proxy ihram] ... And we have already stated that ihram is intention with talbiyah; so if the companion intends and says the talbiyah, the unconscious person becomes a muhrim, not the companion. Hence it is permissible for the companion afterwards to perform ihram on behalf of the unconscious person, even if he is already a muhrim for himself. And the deputy is not required to remove stitched garments for the sake of his ihram on behalf of the unconscious person. And if one performs ihram for himself and on behalf of his companion, and commits a prohibited act of ihram, only one penalty is due to him, unlike the qarin for whom two penalties are due because he is muhrim with two ihrams. And it includes if he performs ihram for him for Hajj or Umrah or both from the miqat or from Makkah, and I have not seen it explicitly. And by 'companion' is meant one of the people of the caravan, whether he is mixing with him or not, as they said regarding the case of fear of thirst for his companion in tayammum that it is the one from the caravan. And it is valid regarding himself prayer, for it is an independent act of worship evidenced by the fact that one may perform it supererogatorily while intention is not a condition for it. This I have not seen from anyone and no answer for it has appeared to me. And it was objected that it is not an independent act of worship due to what al-Qahustani mentioned in l'tikaf: that vowing it is explained by the fact that it was prescribed as a follower of prayer, not for its own essence. His statement: (And I have not seen it explicitly) al-Ramli said: Their general expression indicates it. And in al-Nahr: What is apparent from al-Fath, i.e. from his statement coming soon about one who knows his purpose, indicates that intention is necessary. It is not valid.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling that any moment of wuquf at Arafah from the time of zawal (when the sun passes its zenith) until the dawn of the day of nahr (10th of Dhul Hijjah) renders the Hajj complete, even if the pilgrim was ignorant, asleep, or unconscious. The commentator provides the proof: the Prophet's action (stopping after zawal) and his statement 'Whoever catches Arafah by night has caught the Hajj' define the time boundaries. The word 'sā'ah' (moment/hour) is interpreted as a customary short period, not a precise hour. The 'completion' of Hajj here means security from invalidity, not literal completion, because the second pillar (tawaf al-ziyarah) still remains. A crucial point is that intention (niyyah) is not required for the validity of wuquf, unlike tawaf which requires intention. This is because wuquf is not an independent act of worship (one cannot perform supererogatory wuquf), so the intention from the ihram suffices. The commentary then addresses a difference: if one performs tawaf without intention, it is invalid; but wuquf without specific intention is valid because the general intention of ihram covers it. The discussion then turns to the case of an unconscious person (mugmā 'alayh). The matn states that wuquf is valid even if one is unconscious; the commentator adds that if the companion (rafīq) performs ihram for the unconscious person, it is valid. This is based on the principle of implicit permission (al-istī'ānah dilālatan). The commentator lists numerous furū' (branches) from Jami' al-Fusulayn where similar implicit permission applies: slaughtering a tied sheep, sacrificing another's animal, cooking meat in a pot, transporting grain, etc. The general principle is that for actions where people do not differ significantly (i.e., standard tasks), implicit permission is assumed. For actions that vary greatly among individuals (like skilled slaughtering or flaying), permission is not assumed. The commentary concludes that ihram consists of intention plus talbiyah; if the companion performs these, the unconscious person becomes the muhrim, not the companion. The companion may later perform ihram for himself separately. The deputy is not required to remove stitched garments for the unconscious person's ihram. If someone performs ihram for himself and also for his companion, and commits a prohibited act during ihram, only one penalty is owed (unlike the qarin who owes two). The term 'rafīq' means any person from the caravan, whether or not they are close associates. The passage ends with a discussion about whether intention is always required, referencing prayer (salat) as an independent act of worship, and noting that the author has not seen an explicit statement on some points.

Key Terms
الاستعانة دلالة (al-istī'ānah dilālatan): Implicit permission; the principle that in actions where there is no variation among people, permission is assumed by implication of the circumstances.
المغمى عليه (al-mughmā 'alayh): A person who is unconscious, whether due to illness, injury, etc.
الرفيق (al-rafīq): Companion; in this context, any person in the same caravan, regardless of close association.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what makes proxy ihram for an unconscious person valid?
A) Explicit verbal permission.
B) The companion being a mahram.
C) Implicit permission derived from companionship.
D) The unconscious person's prior intention.

Key Takeaway
Proxy ihram for an unconscious person is valid in the Hanafi school based on the principle of implicit permission (istī'ānah dilālatan), which applies to acts that do not vary among individuals.

The Case of the Unconscious Pilgrim: Implied Permission in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains the ruling of a companion performing ihram for an unconscious person with examples of implied permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on implied permission for a companion to perform ihram on behalf of an unconscious person, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the principle of idhn dalalah to other scenarios.

Core Idea
Companionship can imply permission when a person is unable, but only for tasks where people do not differ.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a traveling companion may perform ihram for an unconscious person without explicit command. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents Abu Hanifa's view that companionship itself grants implied permission (idhn dalalah), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restrict companionship to travel purposes. The commentator then lists numerous examples from Jami' al-Fusulayn to illustrate the broader principle of idhn dalalah. Understanding this dispute helps you grasp how Hanafis derive rulings through istihsan and customary practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed and disputed scenarios
First, note that the base text states: if a companion performs ihram for an unconscious person, it is valid. The commentary clarifies that this covers two cases: when the unconscious person explicitly ordered it (agreed upon) and when he did not (disputed).
The agreed case is straightforward; the dispute centers on the implicit case.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that companionship implies a command by indication (dalalah) when the person is unable. His evidence is the general principle that for actions where people do not differ, assistance is established by indication for everyone.
This is based on istihsan and customary practice ('urf).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that companionship is only for the purposes of travel, not as a basis for authorizing actions on another's behalf without explicit command.
They require explicit permission (idhn sarih).
4
Understand the principle of idhn dalalah through the examples
The commentator lists several cases from Jami' al-Fusulayn where implied permission is recognized, such as slaughtering another's sacrificial animal after it is tied, cooking meat in a pot, and driving a donkey to mill. These illustrate that for routine, non-specialized tasks, assistance is presumed permissible.
The key distinction is between tasks where people do not differ (e.g., tying a sheep) and where they do (e.g., skinning a sheep).
5
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Using the maxim 'every action in which people do not differ, assistance is established by indication,' you can predict rulings for similar situations. For example, if a load falls in the road, a passerby may carry it without liability because the task does not require special skill.
This is a form of qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الزوال إلى فجر النحر فقد تم حجه ولو جاهلاً أو نائماً أو مغمى عليه ولو أهل عنه رفيقه بإغمائه جاز أي أحرم أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان أمره بأن يحرم عنه عجزه زه أو لا، والأول متفق عليه وفي الثاني خلاف أبي يوسف ومحمد بناء على أن المرافقة أمر به دلالة عند العجز عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما إنما تراد المرافقة لأمر السفر لا غير ويتفرع على ثبوت الإذن دلالة مسائل ذكرها في جامع الفصولين منها مسألة الحج، ومنها ذبح شاة قصاب شدها للذبح لا ضمان عليه لا لو لم يشدها، ومنها ذبح أضحية غيره في أيامها بلا إذنه. وذكرها في أكثر الكتب مطلقة وقيدت في بعضها بما إذا أضجعها للذبح، ومنها وضع القدر على كانون وفيه اللحم ووضع الحطب تحتها فوق النار رجل وطبخ لا ضمان عليه ومنها جعل بره في دورق وربط الحمار فساقه رجل حتى طحنه، ومنها سقط حمل في الطريق فحمل بلا إذن ربه فتلفت الدابة، ومنها رفع جرة نفسه فأعانه آخر على الرفع فانكسرت، ومنها مزارع زرع الأرض ببذر ربها ولم ينبت حتى سقاها ربها بلا أمره فالخارج بينهما لأنه لما هيئت للسقي والتربية صار مستعيناً بكل من قام به دلالة، وكذا لو سقاها أجنبي والمسألة بحالها. ومنها من أحضر فعلة لهدم دار فهدم آخر بلا إذن لا يضمن استحساناً والأصل في جنسها أن كل عمل لا يتفاوت فيه الناس تثبت الاستعانة فيه بكل أحد دلالة، وكل عمل يتفاوت فيه الناس لا تثبت الاستعانة فيه بكل أحد كما لو ذبح شاة وعلقها للسلخ فسلخها رجل بلا إذنه ضمن ا.هـ.
From midday to the dawn of the day of sacrifice, his Hajj is complete, even if he was ignorant, sleeping, or unconscious, and if his companion performed ihram on his behalf due to his unconsciousness, it is valid, meaning he performed ihram; it is general and includes the case where he ordered him to perform ihram on his behalf due to his inability, whether [the companion] was instructed or not. The first case is agreed upon, and concerning the second there is a disagreement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad based on the fact that companionship implies a command by indication when one is unable, according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two of them, companionship is intended only for the purpose of travel, nothing else. And derived from the establishment of permission through indication are issues mentioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn: including the issue of Hajj; including slaughtering a sheep that a butcher tied for slaughter – no liability on him, but if he did not tie it, [liability]; including slaughtering another's sacrificial animal during its days without his permission. It was mentioned in most books absolutely, and restricted in some to the case where he laid it down for slaughter; including placing a pot on a stove with meat in it, and a person placing firewood under it and cooking – no liability; including putting his wheat in a container and tying the donkey, then a man drove it until it ground the wheat; including a load that fell in the road, then someone carried it without the owner's permission and the animal perished; including lifting his own jar and another helped him lift it, and it broke; including a sharecropper who sowed the land with the owner's seed and it did not sprout until the owner watered it without his command – the produce is between them because when it was prepared for watering and cultivation, it became that both parties assisted by indication; similarly if an outsider watered it, the same applies. Among them: a person brought workers to demolish a house, then another demolished it without permission – he is not liable by istihsan. The principle of this type is that any action in which people do not differ, assistance is established by indication for everyone; and any action in which people differ, assistance is not established for everyone, such as if a person slaughtered a sheep and hung it for skinning, then another skinned it without permission – he is liable.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of a companion performing ihram on behalf of an unconscious person. The base ruling states that if a companion performs ihram for an unconscious person, it is valid. The commentator elaborates that this includes two scenarios: when the unconscious person explicitly ordered the companion to do so (which is agreed upon), and when he did not. For the second scenario, Abu Hanifa holds that companionship constitutes implied permission due to the unconscious person's inability. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, arguing that companionship is only for the purposes of travel, not for acting on behalf of another. The commentator then provides numerous examples from Jami' al-Fusulayn illustrating the principle of implied permission, such as slaughtering another's sacrificial animal, cooking meat, driving a donkey to mill, etc. The overarching principle is that for tasks where people do not differ in skill, implied permission is established; for tasks where they do differ, permission is not implied. These examples serve as legal precedents (furu') for the general rule.

Key Terms
رفيق (rafiq): One from the caravan (qafila), who may or may not be a close associate; used in the context of traveling companions.
إذن دلالة (idhn dalalah): Implied permission derived from the situation and customary practice, as opposed to explicit verbal permission.
إغماء (ighma'): Unconsciousness; a state that prevents a person from performing actions or giving consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Whether the companion must also be in ihram
D) Whether the unconscious person must be from the same country

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, implied permission (idhn dalalah) can arise from situations like companionship, but it is limited to tasks where people do not differ; the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn illustrates the methodological tension between expansive istihsan and textual conservatism.

Companion's Ihram for Unconscious and Dispute on Tawaf Intention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegation of ihram and necessity of separate tawaf intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a companion to perform ihram on behalf of an unconscious person and explain the Hanafi dispute on whether a separate intention (niyyah) is required for tawaf.

Core Idea
For a valid vicarious ihram, the companion must know the unconscious person's intent; the need for a separate tawaf intention is disputed among Hanafi authorities.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two interrelated issues: the delegation of ihram to a companion for an unconscious person, and the necessity of a separate intention for tawaf. The first part details conditions such as knowledge of the unconscious person's intended worship (Hajj or Umrah) and who may act as deputy. The second part presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Quduri insists on a specific intention at the time of tawaf, while al-Qadi holds that the initial ihram intention suffices. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi scholars reason from analogy and general principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling for vicarious ihram
Note that the companion's intention and talbiyah make the unconscious person muhrim, not the companion. The companion must know the unconscious person's intended act (Hajj or Umrah) for the ihram to be valid.
Knowledge of intent is a condition; if unknown, the companion should choose based on timing.
2
Analyze who can act as deputy
Examine the dispute on whether only a 'rafiq' (caravan mate) can deputize. Fath al-Qadir favors permissibility for any knowledgeable person, based on the principle of assistance.
This affects who may perform ihram when no close companion is available.
3
Examine the dispute on intention for tawaf
Contrast al-Quduri's view (tawaf requires its own intention) with al-Qadi's view (ihram intention suffices). Note the analogy to prayer: Fakhr al-Islam compares tawaf to absentminded prayer actions covered by initial intent.
The disagreement is over whether tawaf is an independent act or a part of the whole Hajj/Umrah.
4
Evaluate the proofs and thamarah al-khilaf
Al-Quduri argues by analogy to prayer (a pillar needing intention); al-Qadi argues by analogy to other rites (sa'y) where initial intention covers. The practical difference: if a person performs tawaf without renewing intention, some deem it valid, others require repetition.
Later Hanafi practice often follows al-Qadi's view, making tawaf valid without renewed niyyah.
5
Synthesize the lesson's contribution
This passage resolves practical questions about deputyship in Hajj and clarifies the flexibility within the Hanafi school on minor rulings. It shows how usul principles (e.g., analogy, necessity) shape fiqh.
Note the use of 'expansion probe' – the text expands on a base ruling with nuanced conditions and disputes.

عبارتهم هنا لبيان الواقع لكن ذكر في المحيط أنه لو أحرم عنه غير رفيقه على قول أبي حنيفة قيل يجوز ، وقيل لا يجوز ولم يرجح، ورجح في فتح القدير الجواز لأن هذا من باب الإعانة الولاية ودلالة الإعانة قائمة عند كل من علم قصده رفيقاً كان أو لا، وأصله أن الإحرام شرط عندنا اتفاقاً كالوضوء وستر العورة وإن كان له شبه بالركن فجازت النيابة فيه بعد وجود نية العبادة منه عند خروجه من بلده، وإنما اختلفوا في هذه المسألة بناء على أن المرافقة تكون أمراً به دلالة عند العجز أو لا ا هـ . ويرجحه أيضاً أن المسائل التي ذكرنا أن الإذن ثابت فيها دلالة لم تختص بواحد معين بل الناس كلهم فيها على السواء، وأشار إلى أنه لو استمر مغمى عليه إلى وقت أداء الأفعال فأدى عنه رفيقه فإنه يجوز وإن لم يشهد به المشاهد ولم يطف به، وصححه صاحب المبسوط لأن هذه العبادة مما تجزيء فيها النيابة عند العجز كما في استنابة الزمن غير أنه إن أفاق قبل الأفعال تبين أن عجزه كان في الإحرام فقط فصحت النيابة فيه ثم يجري هو على موجبه، وإن لم يفق تحقق عجزه عن الكل غير أنه لا يلزم الرفيق بفعل المحظور شيء بخلاف النائب في الحج عن الميت لأنه يتوقع إفاقته في كل ساعة فنقلنا الإحرام إليه بخلاف الميت. وقيد بكونه أغمي عليه قبل الإحرام إذ لو أغمي عليه بعد الإحرام فلا بد من أن يشهد به الرفيق المناسك عند أصحابنا جميعاً على ما ذكره فخر الإسلام لأنه هو الفاعل وقد سبقت النية منه، ويشترط نيتهم منه يحرم العلم بقصده، فإن لم يعلم ينبغي أن لا يجوز له الإحرام بهما بل إما بالعمرة أو الحج، فإن ضاق وقت الحج بأن غلب على الظن أن دخول مكة من الميقات ليلة الوقوف مثلاً تعين الإحرام بالحج وإلا بأن دخلوا في أثناء السنة فبالعمرة لأن الإعانة إنما تكون بما ينفع لا بغيره. وعلى هذا فينبغي أنه لو أحرم بالعمرة والوقت للحج أن لا يصح وهذا فقه حسن لم أر من أفصح به ا هـ، ويرد عليه أن وعلى المؤلف ما في الشرنبلالية أن المسافر من بلاد بعيدة ولم يكن حج الفرض كيف يصح عنه بعمرة وليست واجبة عليه. وقد يمتد الإغماء ولا يحصل إحرام عنه بالحج فيفوت مقصده ظاهراً فليتأمل . ا هـ قوله : (وقد سبقت النية منه وتمام كلامه فهو كمن نوى الصلاة في ابتدائها ثم أدى الأفعال ساهياً لا يدري ما يفعل حيث يجزئه لسبق النية ا هـ . قال في الفتح: ويشكل عليه اشتراط النية لبعض أركان هذه العبادة وهو الطواف بخلاف سائر أركان الصلاة ولم يوجد منه هذه النية ا هـ . قال في النهر : وأقول ما علل به فخر الإسلام مبني على عدم اشتراط النية للطواف أصلاً وأن نية الإحرام مغنية عنه، يفصح عن ذلك ما في البدائع ذكر القدوري في شرح مختصر الكرخي أن الطواف لا يصح من غير نية الطواف عند الطواف. وأشار القاضي في شرح مختصر الطحاوي إلى أن نية الطواف ليست بشرط أصلاً وأن نية الحج عند الإحرام كافية ولا يحتاج إلى نية مفردة كما في سائر أفعال الصلاة. نعم في حكاية الإجماع مؤاخذة لا تخفى وعلى هذا تفرع ما في المحيط : لو طاف
We have already mentioned that ihram is the intention (niyyah) along with the talbiyah. So if the companion intends and recites talbiyah, the unconscious person becomes muhrim, not the companion. Hence it is permissible for the companion thereafter to perform ihram on behalf of the unconscious person even if he himself was already muhrim. And the deputy is not required to remove sewn garments because of his ihram for the unconscious person. And if he performed ihram for himself and for his companion and then committed a prohibited act of ihram, one expiation is required of him, unlike the qarin for whom two expiations are required because he is muhrim with two ihrams. And it includes the case where he performed ihram on his behalf for Hajj, or Umrah, or both, from the miqat or from Makkah, but I have not seen it explicitly stated. And the meaning of 'rafiq' (companion) is one from the people of the caravan, whether he is closely associated with him or not, as they said regarding the case of fearing thirst for his companion in tayammum that 'rafiq' means one from the caravan. Also, it is valid for himself to perform prayer because it is an independent act of worship, evidenced by the fact that one can perform supererogatory prayers, even though intention is not required for it. I have not seen this from anyone and no answer has become clear to me. It is objected that prayer is not an independent act of worship, as al-Qahistani mentioned in I'tikaf that making a vow for it is reasoned by its being prescribed as a follower of prayer, not for its own essence. His statement: 'I have not seen it explicitly' – al-Ramli said: Their general usage indicates it. And in al-Nahr: The apparent meaning in al-Fath, from his statement that will come soon about 'who knows his intention', indicates that it is necessary to [be valid]. Al-Haddadi in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj explicitly stated it. Thus mentioning 'rafiq' in their statements here is to describe the reality. But it is mentioned in al-Muhit that if someone other than his companion performs ihram on his behalf, according to Abu Hanifa it is said to be permissible and it is said not permissible, and he did not give preference. In Fath al-Qadir, permissibility is preferred because this is from the category of assistance and guardianship, and the indication of assistance is present with whoever knows his intention, whether a companion or not. The basis is that according to us, ihram is a condition (shart) by agreement, like wudu and covering the nakedness, although it resembles a rukn (pillar), so deputyship is permissible in it after the existence of the intention of worship from him when he left his country. They only differ in this issue based on whether companionship is a command by indication in case of inability or not. What supports this is also the fact that the issues we mentioned where permission is established by indication are not specific to one particular person; rather, all people are equal in them. And it is indicated that if the unconscious person remains unconscious until the time of performing the rites and his companion performs them on his behalf, it is permissible even if he did not witness the rituals with him or perform tawaf with him. This was confirmed by the author of al-Mabsut because this act of worship allows deputyship in case of inability, like the deputyship of a chronically ill person. However, if he regains consciousness before the rites, it becomes clear that his inability was only in ihram, so the deputyship is valid in that, then he performs the rites himself. If he does not regain consciousness, his inability for all is confirmed, but the companion is not liable for any prohibited act, unlike the deputy in Hajj for a deceased person, because his consciousness is expected every moment, so we transfer the ihram to him, unlike the deceased. It is stipulated that the unconsciousness occurred before ihram; if it occurred after ihram, then the companion must witness the rituals with him according to all our scholars, as stated by Fakhr al-Islam, because he is the doer and the intention has preceded from him. They must intend from him [knowing] his purpose. If he does not know, then it is not permissible for him to perform ihram on his behalf for both; rather, either for Umrah or Hajj. If the time for Hajj is constrained, such that it is most likely that entering Makkah from the miqat on the night of standing (e.g.), then ihram for Hajj is determined; otherwise, if they enter during the year, then for Umrah, because assistance is only rendered by what benefits, not by what does not. Based on this, it should be that if he performed ihram for Umrah when the time for Hajj was [constrained], it is not valid. This is a good fiqhi point that I have not seen anyone articulate. It is objected by the author of al-Shurnubalaliat: How can a traveler from a distant land who has not performed obligatory Hajj be valid with Umrah when it is not obligatory for him? And unconsciousness may extend and no ihram for Hajj will occur for him, so his purpose appears to be missed; let it be considered. His statement: 'and the intention has preceded from him' and the rest of his words: it is like one who intended prayer at its beginning then performed the actions absentmindedly without knowing what he is doing, where it suffices due to the precedence of intention. In al-Fath, it is objected: This requires intention for some pillars of this worship, namely tawaf, unlike the other pillars of prayer, and this intention was not present from him. In al-Nahr, the author says: The reasoning of Fakhr al-Islam is based on the non-requirement of intention for tawaf at all, and that the intention of ihram suffices for it. This is clear from al-Bada'i where al-Quduri in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi stated that tawaf is not valid without intention of tawaf at the time of tawaf. And al-Qadi in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Tahawi indicated that intention for tawaf is not a condition at all, and the intention for Hajj at ihram suffices and does not require a separate intention, similar to the other actions of prayer. Yes, in the narration of ijm, there is a point of criticism that is not hidden, and based on this is what is derived in al-Muhit: if he performed tawaf...
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the companion performing ihram for an unconscious person, now focusing on the conditions and the status of the deputy. It begins by reaffirming that ihram consists of intention and talbiyah, and that the unconscious person becomes muhrim, not the companion. The companion may later perform ihram for himself even if he was the deputy. The deputy is not required to remove sewn garments because his ihram is vicarious. If someone performs ihram for himself and his companion and commits a prohibited act, only one expiation is due, unlike a qarin (combiner of Hajj and Umrah) who owes two. The text then discusses whether the companion must be a caravan member or can be anyone. It mentions a dispute: some say only a companion (rafiq) is permitted, while others—favored in Fath al-Qadir—allow any person who knows the unconscious person's intention, based on the principle of assistance. The unconscious person must have intended some worship before losing consciousness. If he remains unconscious throughout the rites, the companion may perform them on his behalf, and this is valid. If he regains consciousness before the rites, it becomes clear that only the ihram was delegated, and he performs the rest himself. The text notes that if the unconsciousness occurs after ihram, the companion must witness the rituals with him. A crucial condition is that the companion must know the unconscious person's intention (whether for Hajj or Umrah). If he does not know, he should not perform ihram for both; rather, he should choose based on the timeframe. The passage then shifts to a dispute about whether intention for tawaf is required. Fakhr al-Islam compares it to prayer where initial intention suffices for absentminded actions. Al-Quduri holds that tawaf requires its own intention, while al-Qadi says the initial ihram intention is enough. This dispute affects how the companion performs tawaf on behalf of the unconscious person.

Key Terms
مغمى عليه (mughma 'alayh): A person in a state of unconsciousness who cannot perform acts or give consent.
نائب (na'ib): Deputy or agent who performs acts on behalf of another person, here the companion acting for the unconscious.
نية الطواف (niyyat al-tawaf): The specific intention for performing tawaf, whether required additionally or subsumed under the ihram intention.
رفيق (rafiq): Companion from the same caravan, regardless of personal acquaintance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the condition for the companion to perform ihram on behalf of the unconscious person?
A) The unconscious person must have explicitly ordered it
B) The companion must be a blood relative
C) The companion must know the unconscious person's intention (for Hajj or Umrah)
D) The unconscious person must have performed Hajj before

Key Takeaway
The validity of a companion's ihram for an unconscious person depends on knowing his intention, and among Hanafi authorities, a separate intention for tawaf is not universally required; the preferred view is that the initial ihram intention suffices.

Hajj — Pilgrimage
intermediate
10 min
Proxy Ihram for an Unconscious Person
Detailed ruling on proxy ihram for unconscious with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of proxy ihram for an unconscious person and identify the key conditions and the preferred position regarding who may act as deputy.

Core Idea
Ihram and rites for an unconscious person are valid if he had intention, and any informed person can act, not just his companion.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a practical fiqh question: when a traveler becomes unconscious before ihram, can his companion or someone else perform the pilgrimage rites for him? The discussion reveals key Hanafi principles about implied permission (al-idhn dilālah) and the nature of ihram as a condition, not a pillar. It also highlights an internal ikhtilaf within the school that was later resolved by authorities like Ibn al-Humam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is performing ihram and the complete hajj rites on behalf of an unconscious person. The essential condition is that the unconscious person must have had the intention of worship (niyyat al-ʿibādah) when departing from his hometown.
This intention is a prerequisite for any deputyship in worship.
2
Locate the dispute
The text reports an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, there are two narrations—some say only the travel companion (rafīq) can perform the rites, others say any person who knows the unconscious person's intention may do so. The author does not immediately choose, but later cites Fath al-Qadir's tarjih.
The dispute hinges on whether companionship itself constitutes an implied command (amr dilālah).
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof for permissibility
The preferred view (from Fath al-Qadir) argues that the principle of assistance (iʿānah) is general. Implied permission is not limited to the companion; anyone who knows the person's intention can assist because the need is for assistance, not specifically for the companion's action.
This is a qiyās khafī based on the general indication of permission.
4
Note the tarjīḥ
Al-Kamal ibn al-Humām in Fath al-Qadir preferred permissibility, and the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq leans toward this view. The muftā bihī is that any knowledgeable person may act as deputy.
Later Hanafi fatwa works generally adopt this permissibility.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence (thamarāh al-khilāf)
If a non-companion performs ihram, is it valid? According to the preferred view, yes. This expands the pool of possible deputies and makes it easier to fulfill the obligation for the unconscious person.
The opposing view would require the companion to act, potentially causing hardship if the companion is unavailable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صرح به الحدادي في السراج الوهاج فحينئذ ذكر الرفيق في عبارتهم هنا لبيان الواقع لكن ذكر في المحيط أنه لو أحرم عنه غير رفيقه على قول أبي حنيفة قيل يجوز ، وقيل لا يجوز ولم يرجح، ورجح في فتح القدير الجواز لأن هذا من باب الإعانة الولاية ودلالة الإعانة قائمة عند كل من علم قصده رفيقاً كان أو لا، وأصله أن الإحرام شرط عندنا اتفاقاً كالوضوء وستر العورة وإن كان له شبه بالركن فجازت النيابة فيه بعد وجود نية العبادة منه عند خروجه من بلده، وإنما اختلفوا في هذه المسألة بناء على أن المرافقة تكون أمراً به دلالة عند العجز أو لا ا هـ . ويرجحه أيضاً أن المسائل التي ذكرنا أن الإذن ثابت فيها دلالة لم تختص بواحد معين بل الناس كلهم فيها على السواء، وأشار إلى أنه لو استمر مغمى عليه إلى وقت أداء الأفعال فأدى عنه رفيقه فإنه يجوز وإن لم يشهد به المشاهد ولم يطف به، وصححه صاحب المبسوط لأن هذه العبادة مما تجزيء فيها النيابة عند العجز كما في استنابة الزمن غير أنه إن أفاق قبل الأفعال تبين أن عجزه كان في الإحرام فقط فصحت النيابة فيه ثم يجري هو على موجبه، وإن لم يفق تحقق عجزه عن الكل غير أنه لا يلزم الرفيق بفعل المحظور شيء بخلاف النائب في الحج عن الميت لأنه يتوقع إفاقته في كل ساعة فنقلنا الإحرام إليه بخلاف الميت. وقيد بكونه أغمي عليه قبل الإحرام إذ لو أغمي عليه بعد الإحرام فلا بد من أن يشهد به الرفيق المناسك عند أصحابنا جميعاً على ما ذكره فخر الإسلام لأنه هو الفاعل وقد سبقت النية منه، ويشترط نيتهم منه يحرم العلم بقصده، فإن لم يعلم ينبغي أن لا يجوز له الإحرام بهما بل إما بالعمرة أو الحج، فإن ضاق وقت الحج بأن غلب على الظن أن دخول مكة من الميقات ليلة الوقوف مثلاً تعين الإحرام بالحج وإلا بأن دخلوا في أثناء السنة فبالعمرة لأن الإعانة إنما تكون بما ينفع لا بغيره. وعلى هذا فينبغي أنه لو أحرم بالعمرة والوقت للحج أن لا يصح وهذا فقه حسن لم أر من أفصح به ا هـ، ويرد عليه أن وعلى المؤلف ما في الشرنبلالية أن المسافر من بلاد بعيدة ولم يكن حج الفرض كيف يصح عنه بعمرة وليست واجبة عليه. وقد يمتد الإغماء ولا يحصل إحرام عنه بالحج فيفوت مقصده ظاهراً فليتأمل . ا هـ قوله : (وقد سبقت النية منه وتمام كلامه فهو كمن نوى الصلاة في ابتدائها ثم أدى الأفعال ساهياً لا يدري ما يفعل حيث يجزئه لسبق النية ا هـ . قال في الفتح: ويشكل عليه اشتراط النية لبعض أركان هذه العبادة وهو الطواف بخلاف سائر أركان الصلاة ولم يوجد منه هذه النية ا هـ . قال في النهر : وأقول ما علل به فخر الإسلام مبني على عدم اشتراط النية للطواف أصلاً وأن نية الإحرام مغنية عنه، يفصح عن ذلك ما في البدائع ذكر القدوري في شرح مختصر الكرخي أن الطواف لا يصح من غير نية الطواف عند الطواف. وأشار القاضي في شرح مختصر الطحاوي إلى أن نية الطواف ليست بشرط أصلاً وأن نية الحج عند الإحرام كافية ولا يحتاج إلى نية مفردة كما في سائر أفعال الصلاة. نعم في حكاية الإجماع مؤاخدة لا تخفى وعلى هذا تفرع ما في المحيط : لو طاف عنه رفيقه بإغمائه صح. طاف الطواف إذا حملوه كما يشترط نيته وقيدنا بالإغماء لأن المريض الذي لا يستطيع الطواف إذا به رفيقه وهو نائم إن كان بأمره جاز لأن فعل المأمور كفعل الأمر وإلا فلا. كذا في المحيط. فظهر أن النائم يشترط صريح الإذن منه بخلاف المغمى عليه وأنه يشترط نية الحامل للطواف إن كان المحمول مغمى عليه حتى لو حمله وطاف به طالباً الغريم لم يجزه بخلاف النائم لا تشترط نية الحامل له للطواف لأن نية الإحرام منه كافية كما صرح به في المحيط وفيه بحث، فإن الطواف لا بد له من أصل النية ولا يكفي نية الإحرام له كما قدمناه فينبغي أنه لا بد من نية الحامل في المسألتين اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن نية الإحرام لا تكفى للطواف عند القدرة عليها وأما النائم فلا قدرة له عليها. وذكر في المحيط أن استئجار المريض من يحمله ويطوف به صحيح وله الأجرة إذا طاف به وأن المريض الذي لا يستطيع الرمي توضع الحصاة في كفه ليرمي به أو يرمي عنه غيره بأمره ودل كلامه أن للأب أن يحرم عن ولده الصغير والمجنون ويقضي المناسك كلها بالأولى، ولو ترك رمي الجمار أو الوقوف بالمزدلفة لا يلزمه شيء. كذا في المحيط. وذكر الأسبيجابي : ومن طيف به محمولاً أجزأه ذلك الطواف عن الحامل والمحمول جميعاً، وسواء نوى الحامل الطواف عن نفسه وعن المحمول أو لم ينو، أو كان للحامل طواف العمرة وللمحمول طواف الحج أو للحامل طواف الحج وللمحمول طواف العمرة أو يكون الحامل ليس بمحرم والمحمول عما أوجبه إحرامه، وإن طيف به لغير علة طواف العمرة أو الزيارة وجب عليه الإعادة أو الدم ا هـ .
Al-Hadadi explicitly mentioned it in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Then the mention of 'companion' in their expression here is to clarify the reality. But it is mentioned in al-Muhit that if someone other than his companion performed ihram for him, according to Abu Hanifa: it is said it is permissible, and it is said it is not permissible, and he did not give preference. In Fath al-Qadir, he gave preference to permissibility because this is from the category of assistance and guardianship, and the indication of assistance is present for anyone who knows his intention, whether a companion or not. The principle is that ihram is a condition with us unanimously, like wudu and covering the awrah, even though it resembles a pillar. So deputyship is permissible in it after the existence of the intention of worship from him upon his departure from his town. They only differed in this issue based on whether companionship constitutes a command by implication in case of inability or not. And it is also strengthened by the fact that the issues we mentioned in which permission is established by implication are not specific to one particular person, but all people are equal in them. He indicated that if he remained unconscious until the time of performing the rites and his companion performed them for him, it is permissible, even if he did not witness the rites with him and did not perform tawaf with him, and the author of al-Mabsut authenticated it because this worship is one in which deputyship suffices in case of inability, like the deputyship of the aged person. However, if he regains consciousness before the rites, then it becomes clear that his inability was only in ihram, so the deputyship in it is valid, and then he proceeds according to his own requirement. If he does not regain consciousness, his inability for all is realized, but the companion is not liable for anything for committing a prohibited act, unlike the deputy in hajj for the deceased, because [the unconscious person] is expected to regain consciousness at any moment, so we transferred the ihram to him, unlike the deceased. It is restricted to his having lost consciousness before ihram, because if he lost consciousness after ihram, then the companion must have him witness the rites according to all our scholars, as Fakhr al-Islam mentioned, because he is the doer and the intention has preceded from him. And their intention from him... it is necessary to know his intention. If he does not know, then it should not be permissible for him to perform ihram for both [hajj and umrah], but rather either for umrah or hajj. If the time for hajj is tight, e.g., it is predominantly thought that entering Mecca from the miqat on the night of standing, then ihram for hajj is determined; otherwise, if they enter during the year, then for umrah, because assistance is only by what benefits, not otherwise. Based on this, it should be that if he performed ihram for umrah while the time is for hajj, it is not valid, and this is good fiqh that I have not seen anyone articulate. And it is objected that... and the author's statement in al-Shurnublaliyya that the traveler from a distant country who has not performed fard hajj, how can this be valid for him with umrah which is not obligatory upon him? And unconsciousness may extend and no ihram for hajj is performed, so his purpose apparently is lost, so reflect. His statement: 'And the intention has preceded from him' and the rest of his words: 'He is like one who intends prayer at its beginning, then performs the actions forgetfully, not knowing what he is doing, where it suffices him due to the precedence of intention.' It is said in al-Fath: It is problematic because it requires intention for some pillars of this worship, namely tawaf, unlike the other pillars of prayer, and this intention was not present from him. It is said in al-Nahr: I say that what Fakhr al-Islam argued is based on the non-requirement of intention for tawaf at all, and that the intention of ihram suffices for it. This is indicated by what is in al-Bada'i: al-Quduri mentioned in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi that tawaf is not valid without the intention of tawaf at the time of tawaf. And al-Qadi indicated in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi that the intention of tawaf is not a condition at all, and that the intention of hajj at ihram is sufficient, and there is no need for a separate intention, as in the other actions of prayer. Yes, in relating consensus there is criticism that is not hidden. And based on this, what is in al-Muhit is derived: if his companion performs tawaf for him during his unconsciousness, it is valid. Tawaf: if they carry him, as his intention is required. And we restricted it to unconsciousness because the sick person who cannot perform tawaf: if his companion carries him while he is sleeping, if it is by his command, it is permissible, because the action of the commanded is like the command of the commander; otherwise no. Thus it appears that the sleeping person requires explicit permission from him, unlike the unconscious person, and that the intention of the carrier for tawaf is required if the carried person is unconscious, so if he carries him and performs tawaf seeking a debtor, it does not suffice him, unlike the sleeping person, where the intention of the carrier for tawaf is not required because the intention of ihram from him is sufficient, as is clearly stated in al-Muhit. And there is discussion in it: indeed, tawaf must have an original intention, and the intention of ihram is not sufficient for it, as we have presented, so it should be that the intention of the carrier is required in both cases, unless it is said that the intention of ihram is not sufficient for tawaf when one is capable of it, but the sleeping person has no capability for it. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that it is valid for a sick person to hire someone to carry him and perform tawaf for him, and he is entitled to the fee if he performs tawaf for him, and that for the sick person who cannot throw the pebbles, the pebble is placed in his palm so that he throws it, or someone else throws for him by his command. And his speech indicates that a father may perform ihram for his young son and the insane person and perform all the rites by priority, and if he leaves out the stoning or the stay at Muzdalifah, nothing is incumbent on him. Like this in al-Muhit. And al-Asbijabi mentioned: Whoever is carried and tawaf is performed for him, that tawaf suffices for both the carrier and the carried, whether the carrier intended the tawaf for himself and for the carried or did not intend, or if the carrier's tawaf is for umrah and the carried's tawaf is for hajj, or vice versa, or if the carrier is not in ihram and the carried is fulfilling what his ihram made obligatory. And if he is carried without an excuse, tawaf of umrah or ziyarah is required to be repeated or he must offer a dam.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the validity of a companion performing ihram and the complete pilgrimage rites on behalf of an unconscious person. The author, al-Hadadi, comments on the issue of whether only the companion (رفيق) can do this or any person who knows the unconscious person's intention. He reports from al-Muhit that there are two views attributed to Abu Hanifa: one allowing it and one not, with no tarjih. However, in Fath al-Qadir, preference is given to permissibility because assistance (إعانة) is a general principle; anyone who knows the unconscious person's intention can act on his behalf. The principle is that ihram is a condition in Hanafi school, not a pillar, so deputyship is allowed after the unconscious person's own intention of worship at the time of leaving his home. The disagreement is based on whether companionship itself constitutes an implied command (أمر دلالة) when the person is unable. The author strengthens the permissibility by noting that implied permission applies to all, not just a specific companion. He then discusses the case where the unconscious person remains unconscious until the time of rites: the companion's performance suffices even if the person did not witness or perform tawaf. If he wakes up before the rites, it becomes clear his inability was only in ihram, and he continues himself. If he never wakes, his inability is total, but the companion is not liable for any prohibitions, unlike a deputy for a deceased because the unconscious person might wake at any time. This applies only if unconsciousness occurred before ihram; if after, the companion must make him witness the rites. Furthermore, the companion must know the unconscious person's intention; if not, he should choose either umrah or hajj based on the time. If time is tight, hajj is determined; otherwise umrah. If he chooses umrah when time is for hajj, it is invalid. The author then quotes objections and further discussion on the sufficiency of the intention of ihram for tawaf. There is disagreement whether tawaf requires a separate intention or if the ihram intention suffices. The author presents views from al-Bada'i, al-Qadi, and al-Muhit. The ruling of al-Muhit is that tawaf by companion for unconscious is valid. In contrast, for a sleeping person, explicit permission is needed because he can be asked. For the unconscious, no explicit permission is needed. However, the carrier's intention for tawaf is required if the carried is unconscious; if the carrier does not intend tawaf for the unconscious (e.g., he is seeking a debtor), it does not suffice. For a sleeper, the carrier's intention is not required because the sleeper's ihram intention suffices. The author notes a possible inconsistency: tawaf requires its own intention, yet the ruling for the sleeper seems to ignore that. He suggests that perhaps the sleeping person is incapable of intending, so ihram intention suffices. The passage also discusses hiring a carrier for the sick, throwing pebbles on behalf of a sick person, and the father's authority to perform ihram for his minor or insane child. Finally, it quotes al-Asbijabi that if a person is carried for tawaf, that tawaf suffices for both carrier and carried in various combinations.

Key Terms
الرفيق (al-rafīq): The companion traveling with the unconscious person on the same journey.
الإذن دلالة (al-idhn dilālah): Implied permission derived from the situation, e.g., the companion knowing the person's intention to perform hajj.
نية الطواف (niyyat al-ṭawāf): The specific intention to perform tawaf, distinct from the general intention of ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in Fath al-Qadir, can someone other than the companion perform ihram for an unconscious person?
A) Yes, because implied permission is not limited to the companion.
B) No, only the companion can perform ihram for him.
C) Yes, but only if the unconscious person had previously designated that person.
D) No, ihram for an unconscious person is not valid at all.

Key Takeaway
Proxy ihram for an unconscious person is valid based on implied permission, and the preferred view allows any knowledgeable person to act, not just the companion.

Women's Specific Rulings in Ihram
Rulings distinguishing women from men during ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven specific rulings that distinguish a woman's ihram from a man's and explain the Hanafi rationale for each.

Core Idea
A woman in ihram uncovers her face, covers her head, avoids loud talbiyah, ramal, brisk sa'y, and shaving, and may wear stitched clothes.

The Hanafi school establishes that women and men share all obligations of ihram, but women are granted specific concessions and restrictions to maintain modesty and practicality. This lesson unpacks each of these distinct rulings and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
State the General Principle
Identify the rule that women are like men in all obligations of ihram unless specified otherwise.
The text begins with 'والمرأة كالرجل' meaning the woman is like the man.
2
List the Exceptions
Enumerate the specific rulings where women differ: uncovering face, covering head, no loud talbiyah, no ramal, no brisk sa'y, no shaving (instead shortening hair), and permission to wear stitched clothes.
Seven distinct rulings are mentioned in the text.
3
Analyze the Rationale for Each Exception
For each ruling, identify the Hanafi reasoning: modesty (awrah), avoiding attracting attention, practical ease, and following the general principle that commands apply to all unless evidence specifies otherwise.
The text provides some rationale, e.g., head is awrah, face is not.
4
Examine the Inter-Madhhab Difference on Face Covering
Note that the Hanafi ruling to uncover the face is contested by the Shafi'i school which considers the face awrah. Understand the basis of each view.
This is a key point of ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the Rulings to a Scenario
Imagine a woman entering ihram. Describe how she should fulfill each of these rulings in practice.
Consider modern contexts like wearing a loose headscarf and avoiding tight veils.

Source Text

His statement: (And the woman is like the man except that she uncovers her face not her head, and she does not raise her voice in talbiyah, and she does not do ramal, and she does not walk briskly between the two green markers, and she does not shave her head but shortens it, and she wears stitched clothing) because the commands of the Shariah are general for all responsible persons unless there is evidence of specificity. And the reason she does not uncover her head is because it is awrah, unlike her face regarding uncovering the face, and she is unique in covering the head. And since her uncovering the face is hidden... because the apparent sleeper if by his command is permissible, not otherwise. And the intention of the carrier for tawaf is not required because the intention of ihram is sufficient. And he was heedless in al-Bahr and claimed that what is in al-Muhit has discussion because what is in it is based on the non-requirement of intention, so it is not valid to object to it with the opposite statement. And the apparent is that what will come from al-Asbijabi is also derived from that. Reflect on his statement: (And his speech indicates...) Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage presents the specific rulings for women during ihram. The matn states that the woman is like the man in all obligations of ihram except that she must uncover her face (not her head), she does not raise her voice in talbiyah, she does not do ramal (quick walking during tawaf), she does not walk briskly between Safa and Marwah, she does not shave her head but shortens her hair, and she may wear stitched clothing (since ihram clothing for men is not required for women). The commentary explains that the general commands of Shariah apply to both men and women unless there is specific evidence to the contrary. The reason she does not cover her face is because the face is not awrah according to the Hanafi school; however, she must cover her head because it is awrah. The text then becomes garbled and seems to revert to the previous discussion about the sleeping person and the carrier's intention, which may be due to OCR corruption. The clear part of the ruling is that women have distinct permissions and prohibitions in ihram aimed at modesty and practicality.

Key Terms
تكشف وجهها (takshif wajhahā): The woman uncovers her face during ihram; she does not wear a niqab or cover her face.
لا ترمل (lā tarmul): She does not do ramal, i.e., quick walking with short steps in the first three circuits of tawaf.
تلبس المخيط (talbas al-makhīṭ): She may wear stitched clothing (e.g., sewn garments) unlike men who must wear unstitched ihram garments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both head and face are awrah, but covering the face is too difficult.
B) The head is awrah, while the face is not awrah in the Hanafi school.
C) The face is awrah only in the presence of non-mahrams.
D) The head is not awrah, so it can be exposed.

Key Takeaway
Women's ihram rules maintain modesty while providing concessions for practicality, and the Hanafi school uniquely requires uncovering the face.

Tawaf for the Unconscious Person: Carrier's Intention Required
Ruling on tawaf for unconscious carried person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that for an unconscious person carried in tawaf, the carrier must intend the tawaf for him, and identify the consequence of lacking that intention.

Core Idea
Unconscious person's tawaf valid only if carrier intends it for him.

This lesson examines the ruling for a person who is unconscious (mugma 'alayh) during Hajj and is carried by another to perform tawaf. The Hanafi school, as explained in al-Muhit, requires that the carrier have the explicit intention (niyyah) to perform tawaf on behalf of the unconscious person. Without this intention, even if the carrier performs tawaf while carrying him, it does not suffice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and context
The act is tawaf performed by a carrier who is carrying an unconscious person. The key question is whether the carrier's intention is necessary for the tawaf to count for the unconscious person.
The unconscious person cannot form any intention himself.
2
State the ruling from al-Muhit
The carrier must intend the tawaf specifically for the unconscious person. If the carrier performs tawaf for another purpose (e.g., seeking a debtor), it does not count for the unconscious person.
The text gives the example of طالباً الغريم (seeking a debtor).
3
Understand the condition
The condition is the carrier's explicit intention (niyyah) to perform tawaf on behalf of the unconscious person. Without this condition, the tawaf is invalid for the carried person.
4
Contrast with the sleeping person
The sleeping person can give explicit permission before sleeping, so the carrier's intention is not required if the tawaf is done by order. This contrast highlights the unique ruling for the unconscious person.
The principle 'فعل المأمور كفعل الأمر' applies to the sleeping person.
5
Recognize the underlying principle
The requirement of intention for acts performed on behalf of others is based on the inability of the unconscious person to form or express any intention. The carrier's intention substitutes for the missing intention.
This is a case of niyabah (deputyship) where the deputy's intention must be explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طاف الطواف إذا حملوه كما يشترط نيته وقيدنا بالإغماء لأن المريض الذي لا يستطيع الطواف إذا به رفيقه وهو نائم إن كان بأمره جاز لأن فعل المأمور كفعل الأمر وإلا فلا. كذا في المحيط. فظهر أن النائم يشترط صريح الإذن منه بخلاف المغمى عليه وأنه يشترط نية الحامل للطواف إن كان المحمول مغمى عليه حتى لو حمله وطاف به طالباً الغريم لم يجزه بخلاف النائم لا تشترط نية الحامل له للطواف لأن نية الإحرام منه كافية كما صرح به في المحيط وفيه بحث، فإن الطواف لا بد له من أصل النية ولا يكفي نية الإحرام له كما قدمناه فينبغي أنه لا بد من نية الحامل في المسألتين اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن نية الإحرام لا تكفى للطواف عند القدرة عليها وأما النائم فلا قدرة له عليها. وذكر في المحيط أن استئجار المريض من يحمله ويطوف به صحيح وله الأجرة إذا طاف به وأن المريض الذي لا يستطيع الرمي توضع الحصاة في كفه ليرمي به أو يرمي عنه غيره بأمره ودل كلامه أن للأب أن يحرم عن ولده الصغير والمجنون ويقضي المناسك كلها بالأولى، ولو ترك رمي الجمار أو الوقوف بالمزدلفة لا يلزمه شيء. كذا في المحيط. وذكر الأسبيجابي : ومن طيف به محمولاً أجزأه ذلك الطواف عن الحامل والمحمول جميعاً، وسواء نوى الحامل الطواف عن نفسه وعن المحمول أو لم ينو، أو كان للحامل طواف العمرة وللمحمول طواف الحج أو للحامل طواف الحج وللمحمول طواف العمرة أو يكون الحامل ليس بمحرم والمحمول عما أوجبه إحرامه، وإن طيف به لغير علة طواف العمرة أو الزيارة وجب عليه الإعادة أو الدم ا هـ . فاشتركا عن أن هذا قوله : (والمرأة كالرجل الخ (الخ) قال في النهر : لم أر ما لو جن فأحرم عنه وليه أو رفيقه وشهد به كلها هل يصح ويسقط عنه حجة الإسلام أم لا؟ ثم رأيته في الفتح عن المنتقى عن محمد: أحرم وهو صحيح ثم أصابه . عنه فقضى به أصحابه المناسك ووقفوا به فمكث كذلك سنين ثم أفاق أجزأه ذلك عن حجة الإسلام ا.هـ. وهذا ربما يؤمن إلى الجواز فتدبر ا هـ. ولا تنس ما قدمناه قبيل الموقيت فإنه ريح في ذلك
Ṭawāf — if they carry him, tawaf is valid as long as intention is stipulated, and we restricted it to unconsciousness because the sick person who cannot do tawaf, if his companion carries him while he is sleeping, if it is by his order it is valid because the action of the commanded is like the action of the commander; otherwise no. Thus in al-Muhit. So it appears that for the sleeping person explicit permission is required, unlike the unconscious person. And it is required that the carrier intend tawaf if the carried is unconscious; even if he carries him and performs tawaf seeking a debtor, it does not suffice. Unlike the sleeping person, the carrier's intention for tawaf is not required because the intention of ihram from him is sufficient, as stated in al-Muhit, but there is discussion: tawaf necessarily requires a basic intention, and the intention of ihram is not sufficient for it, as we have previously stated. So it should be necessary for the carrier to intend in both matters, unless it is said: The intention of ihram is not sufficient for tawaf when one is capable of it. As for the sleeping person, he has no capability for it. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that hiring a sick person to carry him and perform tawaf is valid, and he is entitled to the wage if he performs tawaf for him. And that the sick person who cannot throw (the pebbles) — a pebble is placed in his palm so he can throw, or another throws on his behalf by his order. And his words indicate that the father may enter ihram on behalf of his young child and the insane person, and perform all the rituals, a fortiori. And if he leaves out the throwing of the jamarat or the stay at Muzdalifah, nothing is incumbent upon him. Thus in al-Muhit. And al-Asbijabi mentioned: 'Whoever is carried and tawaf is performed with him, that tawaf suffices for both the carrier and the carried. It is the same whether the carrier intended tawaf for himself and for the carried or did not intend, or whether the carrier's tawaf was for 'Umrah and the carried's tawaf was for Hajj, or the carrier's tawaf was for Hajj and the carried's for 'Umrah, or whether the carrier is not in ihram and the carried is performing what his ihram necessitates. And if tawaf is performed with him for no reason, the tawaf of 'Umrah or Ziyarah, he must repeat or offer a dam.' Then: 'They both share in that this is his saying: (The woman is like the man etc.) He said in al-Nahr: I have not seen what if he becomes insane and his guardian or companion enters ihram for him and performs all rituals, does it suffice and remove the obligation of Hajj al-Islam or not? Then I saw it in al-Fath from al-Muntaqa from Muhammad: He entered ihram while healthy then became insane, so his companions performed the rituals for him and stood with him, and he remained like that for years, then regained consciousness — it sufficed him for Hajj al-Islam. This perhaps inclines towards permissibility, so reflect. And do not forget what we presented before the miqat, for it is a breeze in that matter.'
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the ruling on performing tawaf for someone who is unconscious (mugma 'alayh) or asleep, when they are carried by another person. The matn likely only said 'Tawaf' and the commentator expands. The main issue is whether the carrier must intend the tawaf for the carried person. According to al-Muhit, if the carried person is unconscious, the carrier must explicitly intend the tawaf for him. If the carrier performs tawaf with the unconscious person for some other purpose (like seeking a debtor), it does not count. For the sleeping person, if he is carried by his order (amr), it is valid because the action of the commanded is attributed to the commander. In that case, the carrier does not need to intend tawaf for the sleeping person because the sleeping person's own intention of ihram is sufficient, since he cannot form an intention for tawaf while asleep. However, the commentator notes a debate: some argue that intention for tawaf is essential and cannot be replaced by the intention of ihram. The resolution is that for one capable, intention is needed, but for the sleeping person, he is incapable. Therefore, the carrier's intention is not required. The passage then covers related issues: hiring someone to carry the sick person for tawaf is valid and the carrier is entitled to wages. The father may enter ihram for his young child or insane person and perform all rituals. There is also a note from al-Asbijabi that if a person is carried and tawaf is performed for him, it suffices for both carrier and carried regardless of whether the carrier intended for himself or not, and even if they have different types of tawaf. Finally, the discussion includes a case from al-Muntaqa about a person who became insane after ihram, and his companions performed the rituals for him; when he regained consciousness, it counted for him. This is a precedent for the validity of proxy performance in cases of insanity. The passage ends with a reference to earlier discussion near the miqat. The lesson highlights the importance of intention (niyyah) in acts of worship and the distinctions based on capacity.

Key Terms
مغمى عليه (Mughman 'alayh): A person who is unconscious or in a state of fainting, unable to perceive or act.
فعل المأمور كفعل الأمر (Fi‘l al-ma'mur ka-fi‘l al-amir): A legal principle: the action of the one commanded is considered the action of the commander. Used here to validate tawaf performed by a carrier on behalf of a sleeping person who commanded it.
نائم (Nā'im): Sleeping person; one who is temporarily unconscious but can potentially be roused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on tawaf for an unconscious person if the carrier does not intend the tawaf for him?
A) The tawaf is valid because the carrier's action covers the carried person.
B) The tawaf is invalid because the carrier must intend it for him.
C) The tawaf is valid because the unconscious person's ihram intention suffices.
D) The tawaf is invalid because the unconscious person is not in ihram.

Key Takeaway
For an unconscious person's tawaf to be valid, the carrier must specifically intend it for him; otherwise it is invalid.

Tawaf for the Sleeping Person: Permission and the Principle of Agency
Ruling on tawaf for sleeping person when carried

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on tawaf for a sleeping person when carried, and apply the principle 'fi‘l al-ma'mur ka-fi‘l al-amir'.

Core Idea
Sleeping person's tawaf valid if by his order; carrier's intention not needed.

This lesson focuses on the ruling for a sleeping person (na'im) who is carried for tawaf during Hajj. Unlike the unconscious person, the sleeping person can give explicit permission before sleeping. The Hanafi school holds that if the tawaf is performed by his order, it is valid even without the carrier's specific intention, based on the principle that 'the action of the commanded is like the action of the commander'. The lesson also touches on the debate about whether the intention of ihram suffices for tawaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
A sleeping person is carried by a companion to perform tawaf. The key is whether the sleeping person gave an order (amr) before sleeping or if the carrier acts without permission.
2
State the ruling with its condition
If the tawaf is by the sleeping person's order, it is valid and the carrier's intention is not required. If without order, it is invalid.
The condition is explicit permission (sarih al-idhn).
3
Understand the applied principle
The principle 'fi‘l al-ma’mur ka-fi‘l al-amir' means the act of the one commanded is attributed to the commander. Thus, the tawaf counts for the sleeping commander.
This principle is from usul al-fiqh, used in agency.
4
Analyze the debate about intention of ihram
Some scholars argue that the intention of ihram alone should be sufficient for tawaf, but the text notes a discussion. The resolution is that the sleeping person is incapable of forming a separate intention for tawaf, so his ihram intention stands in.
The text says 'فيه بحث' (there is discussion).
5
Contrast with the unconscious person
The unconscious person cannot give permission, so his case requires the carrier's intention. This contrast highlights the importance of the ability to command.
The distinction is based on capacity to give order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طاف الطواف إذا حملوه كما يشترط نيته وقيدنا بالإغماء لأن المريض الذي لا يستطيع الطواف إذا به رفيقه وهو نائم إن كان بأمره جاز لأن فعل المأمور كفعل الأمر وإلا فلا. كذا في المحيط. فظهر أن النائم يشترط صريح الإذن منه بخلاف المغمى عليه وأنه يشترط نية الحامل للطواف إن كان المحمول مغمى عليه حتى لو حمله وطاف به طالباً الغريم لم يجزه بخلاف النائم لا تشترط نية الحامل له للطواف لأن نية الإحرام منه كافية كما صرح به في المحيط وفيه بحث، فإن الطواف لا بد له من أصل النية ولا يكفي نية الإحرام له كما قدمناه فينبغي أنه لا بد من نية الحامل في المسألتين اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن نية الإحرام لا تكفى للطواف عند القدرة عليها وأما النائم فلا قدرة له عليها. وذكر في المحيط أن استئجار المريض من يحمله ويطوف به صحيح وله الأجرة إذا طاف به وأن المريض الذي لا يستطيع الرمي توضع الحصاة في كفه ليرمي به أو يرمي عنه غيره بأمره ودل كلامه أن للأب أن يحرم عن ولده الصغير والمجنون ويقضي المناسك كلها بالأولى، ولو ترك رمي الجمار أو الوقوف بالمزدلفة لا يلزمه شيء. كذا في المحيط. وذكر الأسبيجابي : ومن طيف به محمولاً أجزأه ذلك الطواف عن الحامل والمحمول جميعاً، وسواء نوى الحامل الطواف عن نفسه وعن المحمول أو لم ينو، أو كان للحامل طواف العمرة وللمحمول طواف الحج أو للحامل طواف الحج وللمحمول طواف العمرة أو يكون الحامل ليس بمحرم والمحمول عما أوجبه إحرامه، وإن طيف به لغير علة طواف العمرة أو الزيارة وجب عليه الإعادة أو الدم ا هـ . فاشتركا عن أن هذا قوله : (والمرأة كالرجل الخ (الخ) قال في النهر : لم أر ما لو جن فأحرم عنه وليه أو رفيقه وشهد به كلها هل يصح ويسقط عنه حجة الإسلام أم لا؟ ثم رأيته في الفتح عن المنتقى عن محمد: أحرم وهو صحيح ثم أصابه . عنه فقضى به أصحابه المناسك ووقفوا به فمكث كذلك سنين ثم أفاق أجزأه ذلك عن حجة الإسلام ا.هـ. وهذا ربما يؤمن إلى الجواز فتدبر ا هـ. ولا تنس ما قدمناه قبيل الموقيت فإنه ريح في ذلك
Ṭawāf — if they carry him, tawaf is valid as long as intention is stipulated, and we restricted it to unconsciousness because the sick person who cannot do tawaf, if his companion carries him while he is sleeping, if it is by his order it is valid because the action of the commanded is like the action of the commander; otherwise no. Thus in al-Muhit. So it appears that for the sleeping person explicit permission is required, unlike the unconscious person. And it is required that the carrier intend tawaf if the carried is unconscious; even if he carries him and performs tawaf seeking a debtor, it does not suffice. Unlike the sleeping person, the carrier's intention for tawaf is not required because the intention of ihram from him is sufficient, as stated in al-Muhit, but there is discussion: tawaf necessarily requires a basic intention, and the intention of ihram is not sufficient for it, as we have previously stated. So it should be necessary for the carrier to intend in both matters, unless it is said: The intention of ihram is not sufficient for tawaf when one is capable of it. As for the sleeping person, he has no capability for it. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that hiring a sick person to carry him and perform tawaf is valid, and he is entitled to the wage if he performs tawaf for him. And that the sick person who cannot throw (the pebbles) — a pebble is placed in his palm so he can throw, or another throws on his behalf by his order. And his words indicate that the father may enter ihram on behalf of his young child and the insane person, and perform all the rituals, a fortiori. And if he leaves out the throwing of the jamarat or the stay at Muzdalifah, nothing is incumbent upon him. Thus in al-Muhit. And al-Asbijabi mentioned: 'Whoever is carried and tawaf is performed with him, that tawaf suffices for both the carrier and the carried. It is the same whether the carrier intended tawaf for himself and for the carried or did not intend, or whether the carrier's tawaf was for 'Umrah and the carried's tawaf was for Hajj, or the carrier's tawaf was for Hajj and the carried's for 'Umrah, or whether the carrier is not in ihram and the carried is performing what his ihram necessitates. And if tawaf is performed with him for no reason, the tawaf of 'Umrah or Ziyarah, he must repeat or offer a dam.' Then: 'They both share in that this is his saying: (The woman is like the man etc.) He said in al-Nahr: I have not seen what if he becomes insane and his guardian or companion enters ihram for him and performs all rituals, does it suffice and remove the obligation of Hajj al-Islam or not? Then I saw it in al-Fath from al-Muntaqa from Muhammad: He entered ihram while healthy then became insane, so his companions performed the rituals for him and stood with him, and he remained like that for years, then regained consciousness — it sufficed him for Hajj al-Islam. This perhaps inclines towards permissibility, so reflect. And do not forget what we presented before the miqat, for it is a breeze in that matter.'
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the ruling on performing tawaf for someone who is unconscious (mugma 'alayh) or asleep, when they are carried by another person. The matn likely only said 'Tawaf' and the commentator expands. The main issue is whether the carrier must intend the tawaf for the carried person. According to al-Muhit, if the carried person is unconscious, the carrier must explicitly intend the tawaf for him. If the carrier performs tawaf with the unconscious person for some other purpose (like seeking a debtor), it does not count. For the sleeping person, if he is carried by his order (amr), it is valid because the action of the commanded is attributed to the commander. In that case, the carrier does not need to intend tawaf for the sleeping person because the sleeping person's own intention of ihram is sufficient, since he cannot form an intention for tawaf while asleep. However, the commentator notes a debate: some argue that intention for tawaf is essential and cannot be replaced by the intention of ihram. The resolution is that for one capable, intention is needed, but for the sleeping person, he is incapable. Therefore, the carrier's intention is not required. The passage then covers related issues: hiring someone to carry the sick person for tawaf is valid and the carrier is entitled to wages. The father may enter ihram for his young child or insane person and perform all rituals. There is also a note from al-Asbijabi that if a person is carried and tawaf is performed for him, it suffices for both carrier and carried regardless of whether the carrier intended for himself or not, and even if they have different types of tawaf. Finally, the discussion includes a case from al-Muntaqa about a person who became insane after ihram, and his companions performed the rituals for him; when he regained consciousness, it counted for him. This is a precedent for the validity of proxy performance in cases of insanity. The passage ends with a reference to earlier discussion near the miqat. The lesson highlights the importance of intention (niyyah) in acts of worship and the distinctions based on capacity.

Key Terms
نائم (Nā'im): Sleeping person; one who is temporarily unconscious but can potentially be roused.
فعل المأمور كفعل الأمر (Fi‘l al-ma'mur ka-fi‘l al-amir): A legal principle: the action of the one commanded is considered the action of the commander. Used here to validate tawaf performed by a carrier on behalf of a sleeping person who commanded it.
إذن صريح (Idhn sarih): Explicit permission; clear and unambiguous authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the carrier's intention not required for a sleeping person's tawaf if done by order?
A) Because the carrier's intention is automatically transferred.
B) Because the sleeping person's intention of ihram is sufficient due to his incapacity.
C) Because tawaf does not require any intention.
D) Because the carrier's action is considered the sleeping person's action directly.

Key Takeaway
A sleeping person's tawaf is valid if carried by his order; the carrier's intention is not required because the sleeping person's ihram intention suffices due to his incapacity.

Extended Rulings: Hiring, Proxy for Child/Insane, and al-Asbijabi's View
Extended rulings on proxy tawaf and related issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rulings on hiring someone to perform tawaf, proxy for a child or insane person, and the view of al-Asbijabi that tawaf suffices for both carrier and carried.

Core Idea
Hiring for tawaf is valid; father may perform hajj for child/insane; al-Asbijabi says tawaf counts for both carrier and carried.

This lesson covers additional rulings derived from the same passage. It addresses the validity of hiring someone to carry and perform tawaf for a sick person, the authority of a father to enter ihram for his young child or insane person, and the view of al-Asbijabi that when a person is carried for tawaf, it suffices for both the carrier and the carried regardless of intention. The lesson also includes a case from al-Muntaqa about a person who became insane after ihram and whose companions performed the rituals for him.

Guided Path
1
Examine the ruling on hiring for tawaf
The text states that hiring a sick person to carry him and perform tawaf is valid, and the carrier is entitled to wages if he performs the tawaf.
This is based on the principle of ijara (hiring) for acts of worship.
2
Understand proxy for child and insane person
The father may enter ihram on behalf of his young child and the insane person, and perform all rituals. This is a fortiori from the previous rulings.
The text says 'بالأولى' (with priority).
3
Analyze al-Asbijabi's view
Al-Asbijabi holds that when a person is carried and tawaf is performed, it suffices for both the carrier and the carried, regardless of the carrier's intention or the type of tawaf (e.g., Umrah vs Hajj).
This is a broader ruling than the previous one.
4
Consider the exception for tawaf without reason
If tawaf is performed with someone for no reason (i.e., not due to incapacity), then it must be repeated or a dam is required.
This suggests the ruling is specific to those with valid excuses.
5
Review the precedent from al-Muntaqa
A person entered ihram while healthy, then became insane. His companions performed the rituals. When he regained consciousness years later, it counted for his Hajj al-Islam. This is a precedent for proxy in cases of insanity.
This reinforces the permissibility of proxy for the insane.
